<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Amino acid</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the class of chemicals. For the structures and properties of the standard proteinogenic amino acids, see <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic_amino_acid" title="Proteinogenic amino acid">Proteinogenic amino acid</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AminoAcidball.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/AminoAcidball.svg/300px-AminoAcidball.svg.png" width="300" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/AminoAcidball.svg/450px-AminoAcidball.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/AminoAcidball.svg/600px-AminoAcidball.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="702" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AminoAcidball.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The structure of an alpha amino acid in its un-ionized form</div></div></div>
<p><b>Amino acids</b> are <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amine</a> (-NH<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxyl</a> (-COOH) <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional groups</a>, along with a <a href="/wiki/Substituent" title="Substituent">side chain</a> (R group) specific to each amino acid.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The key <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> of an amino acid are <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> (C), <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (H), <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (O), and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (N), although other elements are found in the side chains of certain amino acids. About 500 naturally occurring amino acids are known (though only 21 appear in the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a>) and can be classified in many ways.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> They can be classified according to the core structural functional groups' locations as <a href="/wiki/Alpha_and_beta_carbon" title="Alpha and beta carbon">alpha- <span style="white-space: nowrap">(α-)</span>, beta- <span style="white-space: nowrap">(β-)</span>, gamma- <span style="white-space: nowrap">(γ-)</span> or delta- <span style="white-space: nowrap">(δ-)</span></a> amino acids; other categories relate to <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polarity</a>, <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> level, and side chain group type (<a href="/wiki/Aliphatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aliphatic">aliphatic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Open-chain_compound" title="Open-chain compound">acyclic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aromatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aromatic">aromatic</a>, containing <a href="/wiki/Hydroxy_group" title="Hydroxy group">hydroxyl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, etc.). In the form of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, amino acid <a href="/wiki/Residue_(chemistry)" title="Residue (chemistry)">residues</a> form the second-largest component (<a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> is the largest) of human <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Beyond their role as residues in proteins, amino acids participate in a number of processes such as <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> transport and <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesis</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, amino acids having both the amine and the carboxylic acid groups attached to the <a href="/wiki/Alpha-carbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpha-carbon">first (alpha-) carbon</a> atom have particular importance. They are known as <b>2-, alpha-,</b> or <b>α-amino acids</b> (generic <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">formula</a> H<sub>2</sub>NCHRCOOH in most cases,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> where R is an <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic</a> <a href="/wiki/Substituent" title="Substituent">substituent</a> known as a "<a href="/wiki/Substituent" title="Substituent">side chain</a>");<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> often the term "amino acid" is used to refer specifically to these. They include the 22 <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic_amino_acid" title="Proteinogenic amino acid">proteinogenic</a> ("protein-building") amino acids,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> which combine into <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptide</a> chains ("polypeptides") to form the building-blocks of a vast array of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NIGMS_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIGMS-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> These are all <small>L</small>-<a href="/wiki/Stereoisomerism" title="Stereoisomerism">stereoisomers</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">left-handed</a>" <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomers</a>), although a few <a href="/wiki/D-amino_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="D-amino acid"><small>D</small>-amino acids</a> ("right-handed") occur in <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_envelope" class="mw-redirect" title="Bacterial envelope">bacterial envelopes</a>, as a <a href="/wiki/Neuromodulation" title="Neuromodulation">neuromodulator</a> (<small>D</small>-<a href="/wiki/Serine" title="Serine">serine</a>), and in some <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Twenty of the proteinogenic amino acids are encoded directly by triplet <a href="/wiki/Codon" class="mw-redirect" title="Codon">codons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a> and are known as "standard" amino acids. The other two ("non-standard" or "non-canonical") are <a href="/wiki/Selenocysteine" title="Selenocysteine">selenocysteine</a> (present in many <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> as well as most <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, but not coded directly by <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Pyrrolysine" title="Pyrrolysine">pyrrolysine</a> (found only in some <a href="/wiki/Archea" class="mw-redirect" title="Archea">archea</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Bacterium" class="mw-redirect" title="Bacterium">bacterium</a>). Pyrrolysine and selenocysteine are encoded via variant codons; for example, selenocysteine is encoded by <a href="/wiki/Stop_codon" title="Stop codon">stop codon</a> and <a href="/wiki/SECIS_element" title="SECIS element">SECIS element</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VoJw6fIISSkC_p.299_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VoJw6fIISSkC_p.299-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-url_The_Genetic_Codes_NCBI_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-url_The_Genetic_Codes_NCBI-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/N-Formylmethionine" title="N-Formylmethionine"><i>N</i>-formylmethionine</a> (which is often the initial amino acid of proteins in bacteria, <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a>) is generally considered as a form of <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a> rather than as a separate proteinogenic amino acid. Codon–<a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">tRNA</a> combinations not found in nature can also be used to <a href="/wiki/Expanded_genetic_code" title="Expanded genetic code">"expand" the genetic code</a> and form novel proteins known as <a href="/wiki/Alloprotein" title="Alloprotein">alloproteins</a> incorporating <a href="/wiki/Non-proteinogenic_amino_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-proteinogenic amino acid">non-proteinogenic amino acids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid16260173_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16260173-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pmid19318213_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19318213-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-387-22046-1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-387-22046-1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many important proteinogenic and non-proteinogenic amino acids have biological functions. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a>, glutamate (standard <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamic acid</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Gamma-amino-butyric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma-amino-butyric acid">gamma-amino-butyric acid</a> ("GABA", non-standard gamma-amino acid) are, respectively, the main <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter#Excitatory_and_inhibitory" title="Neurotransmitter">excitatory and inhibitory neurotransmitters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid12467378_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12467378-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyproline" title="Hydroxyproline">Hydroxyproline</a>, a major component of the <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>, is synthesised from <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">proline</a>. <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">Glycine</a> is a biosynthetic precursor to porphyrins used in <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carnitine" title="Carnitine">Carnitine</a> is used in <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid transport</a>.
</p><p>Nine proteinogenic amino acids are called "<a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential</a>" for humans because they cannot be produced from other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a> by the human body and so must be taken in as food. Others may be <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid#Essentiality_vs._conditional_essentiality_in_humans" title="Essential amino acid">conditionally essential</a> for certain ages or medical conditions. Essential amino acids may also differ between <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of their biological significance, amino acids are important in nutrition and are commonly used in <a href="/wiki/Nutritional_supplement" class="mw-redirect" title="Nutritional supplement">nutritional supplements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_feed" title="Animal feed">feed</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Food_technology" title="Food technology">food technology</a>. Industrial uses include the production of <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical drug">drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_plastic" title="Biodegradable plastic">biodegradable plastics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asymmetric_catalysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Asymmetric catalysis">chiral catalysts</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Reactions"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Reactions</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Chemical_synthesis"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Chemical synthesis</span></a></li>

</ul>
</li>

<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first few amino acids were discovered in the early 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In 1806, French chemists <a href="/wiki/Louis-Nicolas_Vauquelin" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis-Nicolas Vauquelin">Louis-Nicolas Vauquelin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Jean_Robiquet" title="Pierre Jean Robiquet">Pierre Jean Robiquet</a> isolated a compound in <a href="/wiki/Asparagus" title="Asparagus">asparagus</a> that was subsequently named <a href="/wiki/Asparagine" title="Asparagine">asparagine</a>, the first amino acid to be discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anfinsen_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anfinsen-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cystine" title="Cystine">Cystine</a> was discovered in 1810,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> although its monomer, <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a>, remained undiscovered until 1884.<sup id="cite_ref-Anfinsen_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anfinsen-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">Glycine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leucine" title="Leucine">leucine</a> were discovered in 1820.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The last of the 20 common amino acids to be discovered was <a href="/wiki/Threonine" title="Threonine">threonine</a> in 1935 by <a href="/wiki/William_Cumming_Rose" title="William Cumming Rose">William Cumming Rose</a>, who also determined the <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a> and established the minimum daily requirements of all amino acids for optimal growth.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The unity of the chemical category was recognized by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Adolphe_Wurtz" title="Charles Adolphe Wurtz">Wurtz</a> in 1865, but he gave no particular name to it.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Usage of the term "amino acid" in the English language is from 1898,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> while the German term, <i>Aminosäure</i>, was used earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Proteins were found to yield amino acids after enzymatic digestion or acid <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>. In 1902, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Emil_Fischer" class="mw-redirect" title="Hermann Emil Fischer">Emil Fischer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Hofmeister" title="Franz Hofmeister">Franz Hofmeister</a> independently proposed that proteins are formed from many amino acids, whereby bonds are formed between the amino group of one amino acid with the carboxyl group of another, resulting in a linear structure that Fischer termed "<a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptide</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_structure">General structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: General structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_Acids.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Amino_Acids.svg/440px-Amino_Acids.svg.png" width="440" height="542" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Amino_Acids.svg/660px-Amino_Acids.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Amino_Acids.svg/880px-Amino_Acids.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="624" data-file-height="769" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_Acids.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 21 <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic_amino_acid" title="Proteinogenic amino acid">proteinogenic α-amino acids</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, grouped according to their side chains' <a href="/wiki/PKa" class="mw-redirect" title="PKa">pK<sub>a</sub></a> values and charges carried at <a href="/wiki/PH#Living_systems" title="PH">physiological pH (7.4)</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the structure shown at the top of the page, <b>R</b> represents a <a href="/wiki/Substituent" title="Substituent">side chain</a> specific to each amino acid. The <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> atom next to the <a href="/wiki/Carboxyl_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Carboxyl group">carboxyl group</a> (which is therefore numbered 2 in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_chain" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon chain">carbon chain</a> starting from that functional group) is called the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_carbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpha carbon">α–carbon</a>.  Amino acids containing an <a href="/wiki/Amino_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Amino group">amino group</a> bonded directly to the alpha carbon are referred to as <i>alpha amino acids</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>  These include amino acids such as <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">proline</a> which contain <a href="/wiki/Secondary_amine" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary amine">secondary amines</a>, which used to be often referred to as "imino acids".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-goldbook_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goldbook-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isomerism">Isomerism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Isomerism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The alpha amino acids are the most common form found in nature, but only when occurring in the <small>L</small>-isomer. The alpha carbon is a <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">chiral</a> carbon atom, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a> which has two indistinguishable hydrogen atoms on the alpha carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-Creighton_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creighton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  Therefore, all alpha amino acids but <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a> can exist in either of two <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomers</a>, called <small>L</small> or <small>D</small> amino acids, which are mirror images of each other (<i>see also <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">Chirality</a></i>). While <small>L</small>-amino acids represent all of the amino acids found in <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> during translation in the ribosome, <small>D</small>-amino acids are found in some proteins produced by enzyme <a href="/wiki/Posttranslational_modification" class="mw-redirect" title="Posttranslational modification">posttranslational modifications</a> after <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Translocation_(genetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Translocation (genetics)">translocation</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">endoplasmic reticulum</a>, as in exotic sea-dwelling organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Cone_snail" title="Cone snail">cone snails</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> They are also abundant components of the <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycan</a> <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a> of bacteria,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and <small>D</small>-serine may act as a <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> in the brain.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <small>D</small>-amino acids are used in <a href="/wiki/Racemic_crystallography" title="Racemic crystallography">racemic crystallography</a> to create centrosymmetric crystals, which (depending on the protein) may allow for easier and more robust protein structure determination.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The <small>L</small> and <small>D</small> convention for amino acid configuration refers not to the optical activity of the amino acid itself but rather to the optical activity of the isomer of <a href="/wiki/Glyceraldehyde" title="Glyceraldehyde">glyceraldehyde</a> from which that amino acid can, in theory, be synthesized (<small>D</small>-glyceraldehyde is dextrorotatory; <small>L</small>-glyceraldehyde is levorotatory).
In alternative fashion, the <a href="/wiki/Cahn%E2%80%93Ingold%E2%80%93Prelog_priority_rules" title="Cahn–Ingold–Prelog priority rules"><i>(S)</i> and <i>(R)</i> designators</a> are used to indicate the absolute <a href="/wiki/Stereochemistry" title="Stereochemistry">stereochemistry</a>. Almost all of the amino acids in proteins are <i>(S)</i> at the α carbon, with cysteine being <i>(R)</i> and glycine non-<a href="/wiki/Chirality" title="Chirality">chiral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Cysteine has its side chain in the same geometric position as the other amino acids, but the <i>R/S</i> terminology is reversed because of the higher atomic number of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> compared to the carboxyl oxygen gives the side chain a higher priority, whereas the atoms in most other side chains give them lower priority.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Side_chains">Side chains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Side chains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lysine_fisher_structure_and_3d_ball.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Lysine_fisher_structure_and_3d_ball.svg/220px-Lysine_fisher_structure_and_3d_ball.svg.png" width="220" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Lysine_fisher_structure_and_3d_ball.svg/330px-Lysine_fisher_structure_and_3d_ball.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Lysine_fisher_structure_and_3d_ball.svg/440px-Lysine_fisher_structure_and_3d_ball.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1147" data-file-height="1047" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lysine_fisher_structure_and_3d_ball.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">Lysine</a> with carbon atoms labeled by position</div></div></div>
<p>In amino acids that have a carbon chain attached to the α–carbon (such as <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a>, shown to the right) the carbons are labeled in order as α, β, γ, δ, and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In some amino acids, the amine group is attached to the β or γ-carbon, and these are therefore referred to as <i>beta</i> or <i>gamma amino acids</i>.
</p><p>Amino acids are usually classified by the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_property" title="Chemical property">properties</a> of their side chain into four groups. The side chain can make an amino acid a <a href="/wiki/Weak_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Weak acid">weak acid</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Weak_base" title="Weak base">weak base</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Hydrophile" title="Hydrophile">hydrophile</a> if the side chain is <a href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar molecule">polar</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobe</a> if it is <a href="/wiki/Nonpolar" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonpolar">nonpolar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Creighton_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creighton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chemical_structure" title="Chemical structure">chemical structures</a> of the 22 standard amino acids, along with their chemical properties, are described more fully in the article on these <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic_amino_acid" title="Proteinogenic amino acid">proteinogenic amino acids</a>.
</p><p>The phrase "<a href="/wiki/Branched-chain_amino_acid" title="Branched-chain amino acid">branched-chain amino acids</a>" or BCAA refers to the amino acids having <a href="/wiki/Aliphatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aliphatic">aliphatic</a> side chains that are non-linear; these are <a href="/wiki/Leucine" title="Leucine">leucine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isoleucine" title="Isoleucine">isoleucine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Valine" title="Valine">valine</a>. <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">Proline</a> is the only <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proteinogenic">proteinogenic</a> amino acid whose side-group links to the α-amino group and, thus, is also the only proteinogenic amino acid containing a secondary amine at this position.<sup id="cite_ref-Creighton_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creighton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In chemical terms, proline is, therefore, an <a href="/wiki/Imino_acid" title="Imino acid">imino acid</a>, since it lacks a <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">primary amino group</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> although it is still classed as an amino acid in the current biochemical nomenclature,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and may also be called an "N-alkylated alpha-amino acid".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zwitterions">Zwitterions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Zwitterions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_acid_zwitterions.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Amino_acid_zwitterions.svg/220px-Amino_acid_zwitterions.svg.png" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Amino_acid_zwitterions.svg/330px-Amino_acid_zwitterions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Amino_acid_zwitterions.svg/440px-Amino_acid_zwitterions.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="378" data-file-height="184" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_acid_zwitterions.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An amino acid in its (1) un-ionized and (2) zwitterionic forms</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zwitterion" title="Zwitterion">Zwitterion</a></div>
<p>The α-<a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acid</a> group of amino acids is a <a href="/wiki/Weak_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Weak acid">weak acid</a>, meaning that it releases a <a href="/wiki/Hydron_(chemistry)" title="Hydron (chemistry)">hydron</a> (such as a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>) at moderate pH values.  In other words, carboxylic acid groups (−CO<sub>2</sub>H) can be <a href="/wiki/Deprotonated" class="mw-redirect" title="Deprotonated">deprotonated</a> to become negative <a href="/wiki/Carboxylate" title="Carboxylate">carboxylates</a> (−CO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> ).  The negatively charged carboxylate ion predominates at pH values greater than the <a href="/wiki/PKa" class="mw-redirect" title="PKa">pKa</a> of the carboxylic acid group (mean for the 20 common amino acids is about 2.2, see the table of amino acid structures above).  In a complementary fashion, the α-<a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amine</a> of amino acids is a <a href="/wiki/Weak_base" title="Weak base">weak base</a>, meaning that it accepts a proton at moderate pH values.  In other words, α-amino groups (NH<sub>2</sub>−) can be <a href="/wiki/Protonated" class="mw-redirect" title="Protonated">protonated</a> to become positive α-ammonium groups (<sup>+</sup>NH<sub>3</sub>−).  The positively charged α-ammonium group predominates at pH values less than the pKa of the α-ammonium group (mean for the 20 common α-amino acids is about 9.4).
</p><p>Because all amino acids contain amine and carboxylic acid functional groups, they share <a href="/wiki/Amphoterism" title="Amphoterism">amphiprotic</a> properties.<sup id="cite_ref-Creighton_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creighton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  Below pH 2.2, the predominant form will have a neutral carboxylic acid group and a positive α-ammonium ion (net charge +1), and above pH 9.4, a negative carboxylate and neutral α-amino group (net charge −1).  But at pH between 2.2 and 9.4, an amino acid usually contains both a negative carboxylate and a positive α-ammonium group, as shown in structure (2) on the right, so has net zero charge.  This molecular state is known as a <a href="/wiki/Zwitterion" title="Zwitterion">zwitterion</a>, from the German <b>Zwitter</b> meaning <i>hermaphrodite</i> or <i>hybrid</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>  The fully neutral form (structure (1) on the left) is a very minor species in aqueous solution throughout the pH range (less than 1 part in 10<sup>7</sup>). Amino acids exist as zwitterions also in the solid phase, and crystallize with salt-like properties unlike typical organic acids or amines.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isoelectric_point">Isoelectric point</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Isoelectric point">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_Acid_Titration_Curves_By_Side_Chain.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Amino_Acid_Titration_Curves_By_Side_Chain.png/360px-Amino_Acid_Titration_Curves_By_Side_Chain.png" width="360" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/Amino_Acid_Titration_Curves_By_Side_Chain.png 1.5x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="390" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_Acid_Titration_Curves_By_Side_Chain.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composite of <a href="/wiki/Titration_curve" title="Titration curve">titration curves</a> of twenty proteinogenic amino acids grouped by side chain category</div></div></div>
<p>The variation in titration curves when the amino acids can be grouped by category.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> With the exception of tyrosine, using titration to distinguish among hydrophobic amino acids is problematic.
</p><p>At pH values between the two pKa values, the zwitterion predominates, but coexists in <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_equilibrium" title="Dynamic equilibrium">dynamic equilibrium</a> with small amounts of net negative and net positive ions. At the exact midpoint between the two pKa values, the trace amount of net negative and trace of net positive ions exactly balance, so that average net charge of all forms present is zero.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> This pH is known as the <a href="/wiki/Isoelectric_point" title="Isoelectric point">isoelectric point</a> pI, so pI = ½(pKa<sub>1</sub> + pKa<sub>2</sub>). The individual amino acids all have slightly different pKa values, so have different isoelectric points. For amino acids with charged side chains, the pKa of the side chain is involved. Thus for Asp, Glu with negative side chains, pI = ½(pKa<sub>1</sub> + pKa<sub>R</sub>), where pKa<sub>R</sub> is the side chain pKa. Cysteine also has potentially negative side chain with pKa<sub>R</sub> = 8.14, so pI should be calculated as for Asp and Glu, even though the side chain is not significantly charged at neutral pH. For His, Lys, and Arg with positive side chains, pI = ½(pKa<sub>R</sub> + pKa<sub>2</sub>). Amino acids have zero mobility in electrophoresis at their isoelectric point, although this behaviour is more usually exploited for peptides and proteins than single amino acids. Zwitterions have minimum solubility at their isoelectric point and some amino acids (in particular, with non-polar side chains) can be isolated by precipitation from water by adjusting the pH to the required isoelectric point.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_functions_in_biochemistry">Occurrence and functions in biochemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Occurrence and functions in biochemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;max-width:372px"><div <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proteinogenic_amino_acids">Proteinogenic amino acids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Proteinogenic amino acids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic_amino_acids" class="mw-redirect" title="Proteinogenic amino acids">Proteinogenic amino acids</a></div> <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Protein_primary_structure" title="Protein primary structure">Protein primary structure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Posttranslational_modification" class="mw-redirect" title="Posttranslational modification">Posttranslational modification</a></div>
<p>Amino acids are the structural units (monomers) that make up proteins. They join together to form short <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymer</a> chains called <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptides</a> or longer chains called either <a href="/wiki/Polypeptides" class="mw-redirect" title="Polypeptides">polypeptides</a> or <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>. These polymers are linear and unbranched, with each amino acid within the chain attached to two neighboring amino acids. The process of making proteins encoded by DNA/RNA genetic material is called <i><a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translation</a></i> and involves the step-by-step addition of amino acids to a growing protein chain by a <a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozyme</a> that is called a <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The order in which the amino acids are added is read through the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a> from an <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">mRNA</a> template, which is an <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> copy of one of the organism's <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>.
</p><p>Twenty-two amino acids are naturally incorporated into polypeptides and are called <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proteinogenic">proteinogenic</a> or natural amino acids.<sup id="cite_ref-Creighton_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creighton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Of these, 20 are encoded by the universal <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a>. The remaining 2, <a href="/wiki/Selenocysteine" title="Selenocysteine">selenocysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrrolysine" title="Pyrrolysine">pyrrolysine</a>, are incorporated into proteins by unique synthetic mechanisms. <a href="/wiki/Selenocysteine" title="Selenocysteine">Selenocysteine</a> is incorporated when the mRNA being translated includes a <a href="/wiki/SECIS_element" title="SECIS element">SECIS element</a>, which causes the UGA codon to encode selenocysteine instead of a <a href="/wiki/Stop_codon" title="Stop codon">stop codon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pyrrolysine" title="Pyrrolysine">Pyrrolysine</a> is used by some <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> in enzymes that they use to produce <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. It is coded for with the codon UAG, which is normally a stop codon in other organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> This UAG codon is followed by a <a href="/wiki/PYLIS_downstream_sequence" title="PYLIS downstream sequence">PYLIS downstream sequence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid16164991_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16164991-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-proteinogenic_amino_acids">Non-proteinogenic amino acids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Non-proteinogenic amino acids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-proteinogenic_amino_acids" title="Non-proteinogenic amino acids">Non-proteinogenic amino acids</a></div>
<p>Aside from the 22 <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic_amino_acid" title="Proteinogenic amino acid">proteinogenic amino acids</a>, many <i>non-proteinogenic</i> amino acids are known. Those either are not found in proteins (for example <a href="/wiki/Carnitine" title="Carnitine">carnitine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamma-aminobutyric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma-aminobutyric acid">GABA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levothyroxine" title="Levothyroxine">levothyroxine</a>) or are not produced directly and in isolation by standard cellular machinery (for example, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyproline" title="Hydroxyproline">hydroxyproline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Selenomethionine" title="Selenomethionine">selenomethionine</a>).
</p><p>Non-proteinogenic amino acids that are found in proteins are formed by <a href="/wiki/Post-translational_modification" title="Post-translational modification">post-translational modification</a>, which is modification after translation during protein synthesis. These modifications are often essential for the function or regulation of a protein.  For example, the <a href="/wiki/Carboxylation" title="Carboxylation">carboxylation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Glutamate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> allows for better binding of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_in_biology" title="Calcium in biology">calcium cations</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and  <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> contains hydroxyproline, generated by <a href="/wiki/Hydroxylation" title="Hydroxylation">hydroxylation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">proline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Another example is the formation of <a href="/wiki/Hypusine" title="Hypusine">hypusine</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eukaryotic_initiation_factor" title="Eukaryotic initiation factor">translation initiation factor</a> <a href="/wiki/EIF5A" title="EIF5A">EIF5A</a>, through modification of a lysine residue.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Such modifications can also determine the localization of the protein, e.g., the addition of long hydrophobic groups can cause a protein to bind to a <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipid</a> membrane.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some non-proteinogenic amino acids are not found in proteins. Examples include <a href="/wiki/2-aminoisobutyric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="2-aminoisobutyric acid">2-aminoisobutyric acid</a> and the neurotransmitter <a href="/wiki/Gamma-aminobutyric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma-aminobutyric acid">gamma-aminobutyric acid</a>. Non-proteinogenic amino acids often occur as intermediates in the <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a> for standard amino acids&#160;– for example, <a href="/wiki/Ornithine" title="Ornithine">ornithine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Citrulline" title="Citrulline">citrulline</a> occur in the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a>, part of amino acid <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a> (see below).<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> A rare exception to the dominance of α-amino acids in biology is the β-amino acid <a href="/wiki/Beta_alanine" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta alanine">beta alanine</a> (3-aminopropanoic acid), which is used in plants and microorganisms in the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Pantothenic_acid" title="Pantothenic acid">pantothenic acid</a> (vitamin B<sub>5</sub>), a component of <a href="/wiki/Coenzyme_A" title="Coenzyme A">coenzyme A</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="D-amino_acid_natural_abundance"><small>D</small>-amino acid natural abundance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: D-amino acid natural abundance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><small>D</small>-isomers are uncommon in live organisms. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Gramicidin" title="Gramicidin">gramicidin</a> is a polypeptide made up from mixture of <small>D</small>- and <small>L</small>-amino acids.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Other compounds containing <a href="/wiki/D-amino_acids" class="mw-redirect" title="D-amino acids"><small>D</small>-amino acid</a> are <a href="/wiki/Tyrocidine" title="Tyrocidine">tyrocidine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valinomycin" title="Valinomycin">valinomycin</a>. These compounds disrupt bacterial cell walls, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Gram-positive" class="mw-redirect" title="Gram-positive">Gram-positive</a> bacteria. Only 837 <small>D</small>-amino acids were found in Swiss-Prot database (187 million amino acids analysed).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-standard_amino_acids">Non-standard amino acids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Non-standard amino acids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 20 amino acids that are encoded directly by the codons of the universal <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a> are called <i>standard</i> or <i>canonical</i> amino acids. A modified form of methionine (<a href="/wiki/N-Formylmethionine" title="N-Formylmethionine"><i>N</i>-formylmethionine</a>) is often incorporated in place of methionine as the initial amino acid of proteins in bacteria, mitochondria and chloroplasts. Other amino acids are called <i>non-standard</i> or <i>non-canonical</i>. Most of the non-standard amino acids are also non-proteinogenic (i.e. they cannot be incorporated into proteins during translation), but two of them are proteinogenic, as they can be incorporated translationally into proteins by exploiting information not encoded in the universal genetic code.
</p><p>The two non-standard proteinogenic amino acids are <a href="/wiki/Selenocysteine" title="Selenocysteine">selenocysteine</a> (present in many non-eukaryotes as well as most eukaryotes, but not coded directly by DNA) and <a href="/wiki/Pyrrolysine" title="Pyrrolysine">pyrrolysine</a> (found only in some <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Bacterium" class="mw-redirect" title="Bacterium">bacterium</a>). The incorporation of these non-standard amino acids is rare. For example, 25 human proteins include selenocysteine (Sec) in their primary structure,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> and the structurally characterized enzymes (selenoenzymes) employ Sec as the catalytic <a href="/wiki/Moiety_(chemistry)" title="Moiety (chemistry)">moiety</a> in their active sites.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Pyrrolysine and selenocysteine are encoded via variant codons. For example, selenocysteine is encoded by <a href="/wiki/Stop_codon" title="Stop codon">stop codon</a> and <a href="/wiki/SECIS_element" title="SECIS element">SECIS element</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VoJw6fIISSkC_p.299_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VoJw6fIISSkC_p.299-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-url_The_Genetic_Codes_NCBI_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-url_The_Genetic_Codes_NCBI-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_human_nutrition">In human nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: In human nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:382px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_acids_in_food_and_blood.png" class="image"><img alt="Diagram showing the relative occurrence of different amino acids in blood serum as obtained from different diets" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Amino_acids_in_food_and_blood.png/380px-Amino_acids_in_food_and_blood.png" width="380" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Amino_acids_in_food_and_blood.png/570px-Amino_acids_in_food_and_blood.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Amino_acids_in_food_and_blood.png/760px-Amino_acids_in_food_and_blood.png 2x" data-file-width="1648" data-file-height="970" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_acids_in_food_and_blood.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of amino acid in different human diets and the resulting mix of amino acids in human blood serum. Glutamate and glutamine are the most frequent in food at over 10%, while alanine, glutamine, and glycine are the most common in blood.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acids" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential amino acids">Essential amino acids</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">Protein (nutrient)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid_synthesis" title="Amino acid synthesis">Amino acid synthesis</a></div>
<p>When taken up into the human body from the diet, the 20 standard amino acids either are used to synthesize proteins and other biomolecules or are oxidized to <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> as a source of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The oxidation pathway starts with the removal of the amino group by a <a href="/wiki/Transaminase" title="Transaminase">transaminase</a>; the amino group is then fed into the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a>. The other product of transamidation is a <a href="/wiki/Keto_acid" title="Keto acid">keto acid</a> that enters the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glucogenic_amino_acid" title="Glucogenic amino acid">Glucogenic amino acids</a> can also be converted into glucose, through <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Of the 20 standard amino acids, nine (<a href="/wiki/Histidine" title="Histidine">His</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isoleucine" title="Isoleucine">Ile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leucine" title="Leucine">Leu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">Lys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">Met</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenylalanine" title="Phenylalanine">Phe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Threonine" title="Threonine">Thr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tryptophan" title="Tryptophan">Trp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valine" title="Valine">Val</a>) are called <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a> because the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">human body</a> cannot <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">synthesize</a> them from other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a> at the level needed for normal growth, so they must be obtained from food.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taurine" title="Taurine">taurine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">tyrosine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arginine" title="Arginine">arginine</a> are considered semiessential amino-acids in children (though taurine is not technically an amino acid), because the metabolic pathways that synthesize these amino acids are not fully developed.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The amounts required also depend on the age and health of the individual, so it is hard to make general statements about the dietary requirement for some amino acids. Dietary exposure to the non-standard amino acid <a href="/wiki/Beta-Methylamino-L-alanine" title="Beta-Methylamino-L-alanine">BMAA</a> has been linked to human neurodegenerative diseases, including <a href="/wiki/ALS" class="mw-redirect" title="ALS">ALS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Holtcamp_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holtcamp-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cox_and_Davis_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox_and_Davis-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

response to physical exercise and specific amino acids or their derivatives (primarily <a href="/wiki/Leucine" title="Leucine"><span class="nowrap"><small>L</small>-leucine</span></a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta-Hydroxy_beta-methylbutyric_acid" title="Beta-Hydroxy beta-methylbutyric acid">HMB</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Skeletal_muscle_homeostasis_2016_review_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skeletal_muscle_homeostasis_2016_review-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Many amino acids derived from food protein promote the activation of <a href="/wiki/MTORC1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skeletal_muscle_homeostasis_2016_review-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_neurology_of_mTOR_75-0" class="reference"><a style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;background:#ccccff">Abbreviations and representations:</div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
&#160;&#8226;&#32;PLD: <a href="/wiki/Phospholipase_D" title="Phospholipase D">phospholipase D</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;PA: <a href="/wiki/Phosphatidic_acid" title="Phosphatidic acid">phosphatidic acid</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;mTOR: <a href="/wiki/Mechanistic_target_of_rapamycin" class="mw-redirect" title="Mechanistic target of rapamycin">mechanistic target of rapamycin</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;AMP: <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_monophosphate" title="Adenosine monophosphate">adenosine monophosphate</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;ATP: <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;AMPK: <a href="/wiki/AMP-activated_protein_kinase" title="AMP-activated protein kinase">AMP-activated protein kinase</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;PGC‐1α: <a href="/wiki/PGC-1%CE%B1" class="mw-redirect" title="PGC-1α">peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor gamma coactivator-1α</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;S6K1: <a href="/wiki/P70S6_kinase" class="mw-redirect" title="P70S6 kinase">p70S6 kinase</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;4EBP1: <a href="/wiki/EIF4EBP1" title="EIF4EBP1">eukaryotic translation initiation factor 4E-binding protein 1</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;eIF4E: <a href="/wiki/Eukaryotic_translation_initiation_factor_4E" class="mw-redirect" title="Eukaryotic translation initiation factor 4E">eukaryotic translation initiation factor 4E</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;RPS6: <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_protein_S6" class="mw-redirect" title="Ribosomal protein S6">ribosomal protein S6</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;eEF2: <a href="/wiki/Eukaryotic_elongation_factor_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Eukaryotic elongation factor 2">eukaryotic elongation factor 2</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;RE: resistance exercise; EE: endurance exercise<br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;Myo: <a href="/wiki/Myofibrillar" class="mw-redirect" title="Myofibrillar">myofibrillar</a>; Mito: <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondrial</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;AA: amino acids<br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;HMB: <a href="/wiki/%CE%92-hydroxy_%CE%B2-methylbutyric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Β-hydroxy β-methylbutyric acid">β-hydroxy β-methylbutyric acid</a><br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;↑ represents activation<br />&#160;&#8226;&#32;Τ represents inhibition </div></div></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:390px;max-width:390px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:221px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Resistance_exercise-induced_muscle_protein_synthesis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Graph of muscle protein synthesis vs time" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/Resistance_exercise-induced_muscle_protein_synthesis.jpg" width="388" height="222" data-file-width="387" data-file-height="221" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Resistance training stimulates muscle protein synthesis (MPS) for a period of up to 48&#160;hours following exercise (shown by lighter dotted line).<sup id="cite_ref-Muscle_hypertrophy_review_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muscle_hypertrophy_review-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Ingestion of a protein-rich meal at any point during this period will augment the exercise-induced increase in muscle protein synthesis (shown by solid lines).<sup id="cite_ref-Muscle_hypertrophy_review_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muscle_hypertrophy_review-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-protein_functions">Non-protein functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Non-protein functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
