</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid_neurotransmitter" title="Amino acid neurotransmitter">Amino acid neurotransmitter</a></div>
<p>In humans, non-protein amino acids also have important roles as <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_intermediate" title="Metabolic intermediate">metabolic intermediates</a>, such as in the biosynthesis of the <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> <a href="/wiki/Gamma-amino-butyric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma-amino-butyric acid">gamma-amino-butyric acid</a> (GABA). Many amino acids are used to synthesize other molecules, for example:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tryptophan" title="Tryptophan">Tryptophan</a> is a precursor of the neurotransmitter <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">Tyrosine</a> (and its precursor phenylalanine) are precursors of the <a href="/wiki/Catecholamine" title="Catecholamine">catecholamine</a> <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitters</a> <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" class="mw-redirect" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">norepinephrine</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Trace_amine" title="Trace amine">trace amines</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phenylalanine" title="Phenylalanine">Phenylalanine</a> is a precursor of <a href="/wiki/Phenethylamine" title="Phenethylamine">phenethylamine</a> and tyrosine in humans.  In plants, it is a precursor of various <a href="/wiki/Phenylpropanoid" title="Phenylpropanoid">phenylpropanoids</a>, which are important in plant metabolism.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">Glycine</a> is a precursor of <a href="/wiki/Porphyrin" title="Porphyrin">porphyrins</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arginine" title="Arginine">Arginine</a> is a precursor of <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ornithine" title="Ornithine">Ornithine</a> and <a href="/wiki/S-Adenosyl_methionine" title="S-Adenosyl methionine">S-adenosylmethionine</a> are precursors of <a href="/wiki/Polyamine" title="Polyamine">polyamines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aspartate" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspartate">Aspartate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Glutamine" title="Glutamine">glutamine</a> are precursors of <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stryer_2002_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stryer_2002-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> However, not all of the functions of other abundant non-standard amino acids are known.</li></ul>
<p>Some non-standard amino acids are used as <a href="/wiki/Plant_defense_against_herbivory" title="Plant defense against herbivory">defenses against herbivores</a> in plants.<sup id="cite_ref-Hylin1969_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hylin1969-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Canavanine" title="Canavanine">canavanine</a> is an analogue of <a href="/wiki/Arginine" title="Arginine">arginine</a> that is found in many <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Turner1967_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner1967-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and in particularly large amounts in <i><a href="/wiki/Canavalia_gladiata" title="Canavalia gladiata">Canavalia gladiata</a></i> (sword bean).<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> This amino acid protects the plants from predators such as insects and can cause illness in people if some types of legumes are eaten without processing.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The non-protein amino acid <a href="/wiki/Mimosine" title="Mimosine">mimosine</a> is found in other species of legume, in particular <i><a href="/wiki/Leucaena_leucocephala" title="Leucaena leucocephala">Leucaena leucocephala</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> This compound is an analogue of <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">tyrosine</a> and can poison animals that graze on these plants.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_in_industry">Uses in industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Uses in industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Amino acids are used for a variety of applications in industry, but their main use is as additives to <a href="/wiki/Compound_feed" class="mw-redirect" title="Compound feed">animal feed</a>. This is necessary, since many of the bulk components of these feeds, such as <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybeans</a>, either have low levels or lack some of the <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a>: lysine, methionine, threonine, and tryptophan are most important in the production of these feeds.<sup id="cite_ref-Leuchtenberger2005_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leuchtenberger2005-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In this industry, amino acids are also used to chelate metal cations in order to improve the absorption of minerals from supplements, which may be required to improve the health or production of these animals.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Food_industry" title="Food industry">food industry</a> is also a major consumer of amino acids, in particular, <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamic acid</a>, which is used as a <a href="/wiki/Flavor_enhancer" class="mw-redirect" title="Flavor enhancer">flavor enhancer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Garattini_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garattini-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Aspartame" title="Aspartame">aspartame</a> (aspartyl-phenylalanine-1-methyl ester) as a low-calorie <a href="/wiki/Artificial_sweetener" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial sweetener">artificial sweetener</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Similar technology to that used for animal nutrition is employed in the human nutrition industry to alleviate symptoms of mineral deficiencies, such as anemia, by improving mineral absorption and reducing negative side effects from inorganic mineral supplementation.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The chelating ability of amino acids has been used in fertilizers for agriculture to facilitate the delivery of minerals to plants in order to correct mineral deficiencies, such as iron chlorosis. These fertilizers are also used to prevent deficiencies from occurring and improving the overall health of the plants.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The remaining production of amino acids is used in the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical drug">drugs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Leuchtenberger2005_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leuchtenberger2005-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, some amino acids derivatives are used in pharmaceutical industry. They include <a href="/wiki/5-HTP" class="mw-redirect" title="5-HTP">5-HTP</a> (5-hydroxytryptophan) used for experimental treatment of depression,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA"><small>L</small>-DOPA</a> (<small>L</small>-dihydroxyphenylalanine) for <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Parkinson&#39;s">Parkinson's</a> treatment,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Eflornithine" title="Eflornithine">eflornithine</a> drug that inhibits <a href="/wiki/Ornithine_decarboxylase" title="Ornithine decarboxylase">ornithine decarboxylase</a> and used in the treatment of <a href="/wiki/African_trypanosomiasis" title="African trypanosomiasis">sleeping sickness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expanded_genetic_code">Expanded genetic code</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Expanded genetic code">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Expanded_genetic_code" title="Expanded genetic code">Expanded genetic code</a></div>
<p>Since 2001, 40 non-natural amino acids have been added into protein by creating a unique codon (recoding) and a corresponding transfer-RNA:aminoacyl&#160;– tRNA-synthetase pair to encode it with diverse physicochemical and biological properties in order to be used as a tool to exploring <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure" title="Protein structure">protein structure</a> and function or to create novel or enhanced proteins.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid16260173_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16260173-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pmid19318213_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19318213-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nullomers">Nullomers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nullomers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nullomers" title="Nullomers">Nullomers</a></div>
<p>Nullomers are codons that in theory code for an amino acid, however in nature there is a selective bias against using this codon in favor of another, for example bacteria prefer to use CGA instead of AGA to code for arginine.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> This creates some sequences that do not appear in the genome.  This characteristic can be taken advantage of and used to create new selective cancer-fighting drugs<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and to prevent cross-contamination of DNA samples from crime-scene investigations.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_building_blocks">Chemical building blocks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Chemical building blocks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Asymmetric_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Asymmetric synthesis">Asymmetric synthesis</a></div>
<p>Amino acids are important as low-cost <a href="/wiki/Feedstock" class="mw-redirect" title="Feedstock">feedstocks</a>. These compounds are used in <a href="/wiki/Chiral_pool_synthesis" title="Chiral pool synthesis">chiral pool synthesis</a> as <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomerically pure</a> building-blocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Hanessian1993_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanessian1993-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Amino acids have been investigated as precursors <a href="/wiki/Chiral_catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiral catalyst">chiral catalysts</a>, e.g., for asymmetric <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> reactions, although no commercial applications exist.<sup id="cite_ref-Blaser1992_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blaser1992-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodegradable_plastics">Biodegradable plastics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Biodegradable plastics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_plastic" title="Biodegradable plastic">Biodegradable plastic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">Biopolymer</a></div>
<p>Amino acids are under development as components of a range of biodegradable polymers. These materials have applications as <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">environmentally friendly</a> packaging and in medicine in <a href="/wiki/Drug_delivery" title="Drug delivery">drug delivery</a> and the construction of <a href="/wiki/Prosthesis" title="Prosthesis">prosthetic implants</a>. These polymers include polypeptides, <a href="/wiki/Polyamide" title="Polyamide">polyamides</a>, polyesters, polysulfides, and polyurethanes with amino acids either forming part of their main chains or bonded as side chains. These modifications alter the physical properties and reactivities of the polymers.<sup id="cite_ref-Sanda1999_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanda1999-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> An interesting example of such materials is <a href="/wiki/Sodium_poly(aspartate)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodium poly(aspartate)">polyaspartate</a>, a water-soluble biodegradable polymer that may have applications in disposable <a href="/wiki/Diaper" title="Diaper">diapers</a> and agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-Gross2002_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gross2002-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Due to its solubility and ability to <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelate</a> metal ions, polyaspartate is also being used as a biodegradeable anti-<a href="/wiki/Fouling" title="Fouling">scaling</a> agent and a <a href="/wiki/Corrosion_inhibitor" title="Corrosion inhibitor">corrosion inhibitor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thombre2005_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thombre2005-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the aromatic amino acid <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">tyrosine</a> is being developed as a possible replacement for toxic <a href="/wiki/Phenols" title="Phenols">phenols</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bisphenol_A" title="Bisphenol A">bisphenol A</a> in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Polycarbonate" title="Polycarbonate">polycarbonates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bourke2003_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bourke2003-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions">Reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As amino acids have both a primary <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amine</a> group and a primary <a href="/wiki/Carboxyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Carboxyl">carboxyl</a> group, these chemicals can undergo most of the reactions associated with these functional groups. These include <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_addition" title="Nucleophilic addition">nucleophilic addition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amide bond</a> formation, and <a href="/wiki/Alkylimino-de-oxo-bisubstitution" title="Alkylimino-de-oxo-bisubstitution">imine formation</a> for the amine group, and <a href="/wiki/Esterification" class="mw-redirect" title="Esterification">esterification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amide bond</a> formation, and <a href="/wiki/Decarboxylation" title="Decarboxylation">decarboxylation</a> for the carboxylic acid group.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The combination of these functional groups allow amino acids to be effective polydentate ligands for metal-amino acid chelates.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
The multiple side chains of amino acids can also undergo chemical reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The types of these reactions are determined by the groups on these side chains and are, therefore, different between the various types of amino acid.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_synthesis">Chemical synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Chemical synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis_scheme.svg" class="image"><img alt="For the steps in the reaction, see the text." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis_scheme.svg/400px-Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis_scheme.svg.png" width="400" height="89" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis_scheme.svg/600px-Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis_scheme.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis_scheme.svg/800px-Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis_scheme.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="469" data-file-height="104" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis_scheme.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Strecker amino acid synthesis</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Chemical_synthesis_of_amino_acids" title="Category:Chemical synthesis of amino acids">Category:Chemical synthesis of amino acids</a></div>
<p>Several methods exist to synthesize amino acids. One of the oldest methods begins with the <a href="/wiki/Hell-Volhard-Zelinsky_halogenation" class="mw-redirect" title="Hell-Volhard-Zelinsky halogenation">bromination</a> at the α-carbon of a carboxylic acid. <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_substitution" title="Nucleophilic substitution">Nucleophilic substitution</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> then converts the alkyl bromide to the amino acid.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In alternative fashion, the <a href="/wiki/Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis" title="Strecker amino acid synthesis">Strecker amino acid synthesis</a> involves the treatment of an aldehyde with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cyanide" title="Potassium cyanide">potassium cyanide</a> and ammonia, this produces an α-amino nitrile as an intermediate. Hydrolysis of the nitrile in acid then yields an α-amino acid.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Using ammonia or ammonium salts in this reaction gives unsubstituted amino acids, whereas substituting primary and secondary amines will yield substituted amino acids.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Likewise, using <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>, instead of aldehydes, gives α,α-disubstituted amino acids.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The classical synthesis gives <a href="/wiki/Racemic_mixture" title="Racemic mixture">racemic mixtures</a> of α-amino acids as products, but several alternative procedures using asymmetric auxiliaries<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> or asymmetric catalysts<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> have been developed.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the current time, the most-adopted method is an <a href="/wiki/Automated" class="mw-redirect" title="Automated">automated</a> synthesis on a solid support (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Polystyrene" title="Polystyrene">polystyrene</a> beads), using <a href="/wiki/Protecting_group" title="Protecting group">protecting groups</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Fmoc_chloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Fmoc chloride">Fmoc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tert-butyloxycarbonyl_protecting_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Tert-butyloxycarbonyl protecting group">t-Boc</a>) and activating groups (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Dicyclohexylcarbodiimide" class="mw-redirect" title="Dicyclohexylcarbodiimide">DCC</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diisopropylcarbodiimide" class="mw-redirect" title="Diisopropylcarbodiimide">DIC</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peptide_bond_formation">Peptide bond formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Peptide bond formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Peptide_synthesis" title="Peptide synthesis">Peptide synthesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">Peptide bond</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peptidformationball.svg" class="image"><img alt="Two amino acids are shown next to each other. One loses a hydrogen and oxygen from its carboxyl group (COOH) and the other loses a hydrogen from its amino group (NH2). This reaction produces a molecule of water (H2O) and two amino acids joined by a peptide bond (-CO-NH-). The two joined amino acids are called a dipeptide." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Peptidformationball.svg/400px-Peptidformationball.svg.png" width="400" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Peptidformationball.svg/600px-Peptidformationball.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Peptidformationball.svg/800px-Peptidformationball.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="990" data-file-height="820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peptidformationball.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The condensation of two amino acids to form a <i><a href="/wiki/Dipeptide" title="Dipeptide">dipeptide</a></i> through a <i><a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bond</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>As both the amine and carboxylic acid groups of amino acids can react to form amide bonds, one amino acid molecule can react with another and become joined through an amide linkage. This <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> of amino acids is what creates proteins. This <a href="/wiki/Condensation_reaction" title="Condensation reaction">condensation reaction</a> yields the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bond</a> and a molecule of water. In cells, this reaction does not occur directly; instead, the amino acid is first activated by attachment to a <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> molecule through an <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">ester</a> bond. This aminoacyl-tRNA is produced in an <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>-dependent reaction carried out by an <a href="/wiki/Aminoacyl_tRNA_synthetase" title="Aminoacyl tRNA synthetase">aminoacyl tRNA synthetase</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> This aminoacyl-tRNA is then a substrate for the <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>, which catalyzes the attack of the amino group of the elongating protein chain on the ester bond.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> As a result of this mechanism, all proteins made by ribosomes are synthesized starting at their N-terminus and moving toward their C-terminus.
</p><p>However, not all peptide bonds are formed in this way. In a few cases, peptides are synthesized by specific enzymes. For example, the tripeptide <a href="/wiki/Glutathione" title="Glutathione">glutathione</a> is an essential part of the defenses of cells against oxidative stress. This peptide is synthesized in two steps from free amino acids.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> In the first step, <a href="/wiki/Gamma-glutamylcysteine_synthetase" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma-glutamylcysteine synthetase">gamma-glutamylcysteine synthetase</a> condenses <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamic acid</a> through a peptide bond formed between the side chain carboxyl of the glutamate (the gamma carbon of this side chain) and the amino group of the cysteine. This dipeptide is then condensed with <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a> by <a href="/wiki/Glutathione_synthetase" title="Glutathione synthetase">glutathione synthetase</a> to form glutathione.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In chemistry, peptides are synthesized by a variety of reactions. One of the most-used in <a href="/wiki/Peptide_synthesis" title="Peptide synthesis">solid-phase peptide synthesis</a> uses the aromatic oxime derivatives of amino acids as activated units. These are added in sequence onto the growing peptide chain, which is attached to a solid resin support.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The ability to easily synthesize vast numbers of different peptides by varying the types and order of amino acids (using <a href="/wiki/Combinatorial_chemistry" title="Combinatorial chemistry">combinatorial chemistry</a>) has made peptide synthesis particularly important in creating libraries of peptides for use in drug discovery through <a href="/wiki/High-throughput_screening" title="High-throughput screening">high-throughput screening</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biosynthesis">Biosynthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Biosynthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid_synthesis" title="Amino acid synthesis">Amino acid synthesis</a></div>
<p>In plants, nitrogen is first assimilated into organic compounds in the form of <a href="/wiki/Glutamate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a>, formed from alpha-ketoglutarate and ammonia in the mitochondrion. In order to form other amino acids, the plant uses <a href="/wiki/Transaminase" title="Transaminase">transaminases</a> to move the amino group to another alpha-keto carboxylic acid. For example, aspartate aminotransferase converts glutamate and oxaloacetate to alpha-ketoglutarate and aspartate.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Other organisms use transaminases for amino acid synthesis, too.
</p><p>Nonstandard amino acids are usually formed through modifications to standard amino acids. For example, <a href="/wiki/Homocysteine" title="Homocysteine">homocysteine</a> is formed through the <a href="/wiki/Transsulfuration_pathway" title="Transsulfuration pathway">transsulfuration pathway</a> or by the demethylation of methionine via the intermediate metabolite <a href="/wiki/S-adenosyl_methionine" class="mw-redirect" title="S-adenosyl methionine">S-adenosyl methionine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Brosnan_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brosnan-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyproline" title="Hydroxyproline">hydroxyproline</a> is made by a <a href="/wiki/Posttranslational_modification" class="mw-redirect" title="Posttranslational modification">posttranslational modification</a> of <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">proline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">Microorganisms</a> and plants can synthesize many uncommon amino acids. For example, some microbes make <a href="/wiki/2-aminoisobutyric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="2-aminoisobutyric acid">2-aminoisobutyric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lanthionine" title="Lanthionine">lanthionine</a>, which is a sulfide-bridged derivative of alanine. Both of these amino acids are found in peptidic <a href="/wiki/Lantibiotics" title="Lantibiotics">lantibiotics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Alamethicin" title="Alamethicin">alamethicin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> However, in plants, <a href="/wiki/1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid">1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid</a> is a small disubstituted cyclic amino acid that is a key intermediate in the production of the plant hormone <a href="/wiki/Ethylene#Ethylene_as_a_plant_hormone" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catabolism">Catabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Catabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_acid_catabolism_revised.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Amino_acid_catabolism_revised.png/300px-Amino_acid_catabolism_revised.png" width="300" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Amino_acid_catabolism_revised.png/450px-Amino_acid_catabolism_revised.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Amino_acid_catabolism_revised.png/600px-Amino_acid_catabolism_revised.png 2x" data-file-width="906" data-file-height="636" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amino_acid_catabolism_revised.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Catabolism of proteinogenic amino acids. Amino acids can be classified according to the properties of their main products as either of the following:<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> <br />* <i>Glucogenic</i>, with the products having the ability to form <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> by <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a> <br />* <i>Ketogenic</i>, with the products not having the ability to form glucose. These products may still be used for <a href="/wiki/Ketogenesis" title="Ketogenesis">ketogenesis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lipid_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Lipid synthesis">lipid synthesis</a>. <br />* Amino acids catabolized into both glucogenic and ketogenic products.</div></div></div>
<p>Amino acids must first pass out of organelles and cells into blood circulation via <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid_transporter" title="Amino acid transporter">amino acid transporters</a>, since the amine and carboxylic acid groups are typically ionized. Degradation of an amino acid, occurring in the liver and kidneys, often involves <a href="/wiki/Deamination" title="Deamination">deamination</a> by moving its amino group to alpha-ketoglutarate, forming <a href="/wiki/Glutamate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a>. This process involves transaminases, often the same as those used in amination during synthesis. In many vertebrates, the amino group is then removed through the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a> and is excreted in the form of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>. However, amino acid degradation can produce <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a> or ammonia instead. For example, <a href="/wiki/Serine_dehydratase" title="Serine dehydratase">serine dehydratase</a> converts serine to pyruvate and ammonia.<sup id="cite_ref-Stryer_2002_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stryer_2002-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> After removal of one or more amino groups, the remainder of the molecule can sometimes be used to synthesize new amino acids, or it can be used for energy by entering <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>, as detailed in image at right.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physicochemical_properties_of_amino_acids">Physicochemical properties of amino acids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Physicochemical properties of amino acids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The 20 amino acids encoded directly by the genetic code can be divided into several groups based on their properties. Important factors are charge, <a href="/wiki/Hydrophile" title="Hydrophile">hydrophilicity</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobicity</a>, size, and functional groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Creighton_38-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creighton-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> These properties are important for <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure" title="Protein structure">protein structure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein%E2%80%93protein_interaction" title="Protein–protein interaction">protein–protein interactions</a>. The water-soluble proteins tend to have their hydrophobic residues (Leu, Ile, Val, Phe, and Trp) buried in the middle of the protein, whereas hydrophilic side chains are exposed to the aqueous solvent. (Note that in <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, a residue refers to a specific <a href="/wiki/Monomer" title="Monomer">monomer</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymeric chain</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acid</a>.) The <a href="/wiki/Integral_membrane_protein" title="Integral membrane protein">integral membrane proteins</a> tend to have outer rings of exposed <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> amino acids that anchor them into the <a href="/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">lipid bilayer</a>. In the case part-way between these two extremes, some <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_membrane_protein" title="Peripheral membrane protein">peripheral membrane proteins</a> have a patch of hydrophobic amino acids on their surface that locks onto the membrane. In similar fashion, proteins that have to bind to positively charged molecules have surfaces rich with negatively charged amino acids like <a href="/wiki/Glutamate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aspartate" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspartate">aspartate</a>, while proteins binding to negatively charged molecules have surfaces rich with positively charged chains like <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arginine" title="Arginine">arginine</a>. There are different <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobicity_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrophobicity scale">hydrophobicity scales</a> of amino acid residues.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some amino acids have special properties such as <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a>, that can form covalent <a href="/wiki/Disulfide_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Disulfide bond">disulfide bonds</a> to other cysteine residues, <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">proline</a> that forms <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_compound" title="Cyclic compound">a cycle</a> to the polypeptide backbone, and <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a> that is more flexible than other amino acids.
</p><p>Many proteins undergo a range of <a href="/wiki/Posttranslational_modification" class="mw-redirect" title="Posttranslational modification">posttranslational modifications</a>, when additional chemical groups are attached to the amino acids in proteins. Some modifications can produce hydrophobic <a href="/wiki/Lipoprotein" title="Lipoprotein">lipoproteins</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> or hydrophilic <a href="/wiki/Glycoprotein" title="Glycoprotein">glycoproteins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> These type of modification allow the reversible targeting of a protein to a membrane. For example, the addition and removal of the fatty acid <a href="/wiki/Palmitic_acid" title="Palmitic acid">palmitic acid</a> to cysteine residues in some signaling proteins causes the proteins to attach and then detach from cell membranes.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Table_of_standard_amino_acid_abbreviations_and_properties">Table of standard amino acid abbreviations and properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amino_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Table of standard amino acid abbreviations and properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>In addition to the specific amino acid codes, placeholders are used in cases where <a href="/wiki/Protein_sequencing" title="Protein sequencing">chemical</a> or <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">crystallographic</a> analysis of a peptide or protein cannot conclusively determine the identity of a residue. They are also used to summarise <a href="/wiki/Conserved_sequence" title="Conserved sequence">conserved protein sequence</a> motifs. The use of single letters to indicate sets of similar residues is similar to the use of <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_notation" title="Nucleic acid notation">abbreviation codes for degenerate bases</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p><b>Unk</b> is sometimes used instead of <b>Xaa</b>, but is less standard.
</p><p>In addition, many <a href="/wiki/Non-proteinogenic_amino_acids" title="Non-proteinogenic amino acids">non-standard amino acids</a> have a specific code. For example, several peptide drugs, such as <a href="/wiki/Bortezomib" title="Bortezomib">Bortezomib</a> and <a href="/wiki/MG132" title="MG132">MG132</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Peptide_synthesis" title="Peptide synthesis">artificially synthesized</a> and retain their <a href="/wiki/Protecting_group" title="Protecting group">protecting groups</a>, which have specific codes. Bortezomib is <a href="/wiki/Pyrazinoic_acid" title="Pyrazinoic acid">Pyz</a>-Phe-boroLeu, and MG132 is <a href="/wiki/Carboxybenzyl" title="Carboxybenzyl">Z</a>-Leu-Leu-Leu-al. To aid in the analysis of protein structure, <a href="/wiki/Photo-reactive_amino_acid_analog" title="Photo-reactive amino acid analog">photo-reactive amino acid analogs</a> are available. These include <a href="/w/index.php?title=Photoleucine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Photoleucine (page does not exist)">photoleucine</a> (<b>pLeu</b>) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Photomethionine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Photomethionine (page does not exist)">photomethionine</a> (<b>pMet</b>).<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 33em; -webkit-column-width: 33em; column-width: 33em; list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">Proline</a> is an exception to this general formula.
